Evolutionary psychology

  • Romantic priming, that looking at attractive women increases men‚Äôs conspicuous consumption, time discount, risk-taking. Weak, despite there being 43 independent confirmatory studies!: one of the strongest publication biases / p-hacking ever found.

    Statistics

    • Original paper: ‘Do pretty women inspire men to discount the future?’, Wilson and Daly 2003. n=209 (but only n=52 for each cell in the 2x2) (~560 citations).
    • Critiques: Shanks et al (2015): show that the 43 previous studies have an unbelievably bad funnel plot. They also run 8 failed replications. (total citations: ~80)
    • Original effect size: d=0.55 [-0.04, 1.13] for the difference between men and women. Meta-analytic d= 0.57 [0.49, 0.65]
    • Replication effect size: 0.00 [-0.12, 0.11]

  • Implicit religious priming. Implicitly priming god concepts by unscrambling sentences with words relating to religion increases prosocial behaviour in an anonymous economic game.

    Statistics

    • Status: not replicated
    • Original paper: ‚ÄòGod is watching you: Priming god concepts increases prosocial behavior in an anonymous economic game‚Äô, Shariff & Norenzayan 2007; 2 experiments, ns=50 in each experiment. [citations=1473 (GS, November 2021)].
    • Critiques: Billingsley et al. 2018 [n=489 and n=511, (citations=22 (GS, November 2021)]. Gervais et al. 2022 [experiment 1a n=556, experiment 1b n=548, citations=9 (GS, November 2022)]. Gomes and McCullough 2015 [n=455, citations=86 (GS, November 2021)]. Kupor et al. 2015 [experiment 1a, n=61, and experiment 1b, n=202, citations=76 (GS, November 2022)]. Snell et al. 2022 [meta analysis, N = 11330, citations = 1 (GS, July 2023)].
    • Original effect size: Study 1, d=1.07; Study 2, d=0.71.
    • Replication effect size: Billingsley et al.: Study 1: g = -0.05; Study 2: g= -0.09. Gervais et al.: experiment 1a d=0.14 [-0.07, 0.34]; experiment 1b, d=-0.11 [-0.31, 0.09]. Gomes and McCullough: d=-0.06. Kupor et al.: experiment 1a d=0.574 [0.05, 1.09]; experiment 1b d=0.323 [0.04, 0.60]. Snell et al.: r = .06. All reported in Snell et al.: Johnson et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.13 [-0.09, 0.36], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.33 [0.06, 0.60]. LaBouff et al.: r=0.29 [0.10, 0.47]. Johnson et al.: r=0.29 [0.08, 0.51]. Clobert et al.: Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.04 [-0.18, 0.27], Study 3 ‚Äì r=0.12 [-0.09, 0.34]. Howard & Sommers: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.05 [-0.14, 0.24], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.12 [-0.02, 0.26]. Ramsay et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.01 [-0.12, 0.14], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.03 [-0.16, 0.23]. Haggard et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.10 [-0.01, 0.22], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.21 [0.03, 0.39], Study 3 ‚Äì r=-0.08 [-0.25, 0.08], Study 4 ‚Äì r=0.13 [-0.04, 0.30]. LaBouff: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.01 [-0.16, 0.18], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.13 [-0.08, 0.33]. Shamoa-Nir & Razpurker-Apfeld: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.03 [-0.26, 0.32], Study 2 ‚Äì r=-0.22 [-0.51, 0.07], Study 3a ‚Äì r=0.04 [-0.18, 0.26]. Johnson: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.03 [-0.11, 0.18], Study 2 ‚Äì r=-0.01 [-0.17, 0.14]. Pope: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.20 [0.02, 0.38], Study 2 ‚Äì r=0.06 [-0.14, 0.26]. Rutchick: r=0.07 [0.01, 0.12]. Basset & Van Tongeren: r=0.01 [-0.07, 0.08]. Erum & Tsang: r=-0.07 [-0.27, 0.13]. Al-Kire: Study 1 ‚Äì r=0.27 [0.07, 0.47]. Ratchford & Al-Kire: Study 1 ‚Äì r=-0.02 [-0.11, 0.08]. Shamoa-Nir & Razpurker-Apfeld: Study 3b ‚Äì r=0.11 [-0.01, 0.23]. Jennings: r=-0.01 [-0.11, 0.09]. Dolinska et al.:_ r_=0.18 [0.06, 0.29]. Razpurker-Apfeld & Shamoa-Nir: _r_=0.14 [-0.06, 0.33]. Ginges et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì _r_=0.05 [-0.02, 0.12], Study 2 ‚Äì _r_=0.01 [-0.06, 0.08], Study 3 ‚Äì _r_=0.01 [-0.04, 0.05]. Yilmaz et al.: _r_=0.30 [0.11, 0.50]. Bloom et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì _r_=0.04 [-0.05, 0.14]. Deel: Study 1 ‚Äì _r_=-0.13 [-0.30, 0.04]. Gilad & Stepanova: Study 1_ _‚Äì_ r_=0.02 [-0.14, 0.19]. Howard: Study 1_ _‚Äì_ r_=0.10 [-0.08, 0.28], Study 2b ‚Äì _r_=-0.08 [-0.25, 0.10], Study 4 ‚Äì _r_=-0.02 [-0.28, 0.23]. Van Tongeren et al.: Study 1 ‚Äì_ r_=-0.04 [-0.33, 0.26]. Wilkins et al.: Study 2_ _‚Äì_ r_=0.08 [-0.03, 0.20].

  • Implicit analytic priming, that implicitly priming analytic thinking by seeing a photo of Auguste Rodin’s The Thinker decreases belief in God.

    Statistics

    • Status: not replicated
    • Original paper: ‚ÄòAnalytic thinking promotes religious disbelief‚Äô, Gervais and Norenzayan 2012; n=57 [citation=601 (Google Scholar, December 2021)].
    • Critiques: Sanchez et al [n=941, citations=59 (Google Scholar, December 2021)]. Camerer et al 2018; 2 experiments, n=224 and n=531 [citations=871 (Google Scholar, December 2021)].
    • Original effect size: d=-0.25 to d=0.12.
    • Replication effect size: Sanchez et al 2017, d=-0.25 to d=0.12. Camerer et al 2018, study 1 r=-0.055, study 2 r=-0.035.

  • Menstrual cycle version of the dual-mating-strategy hypothesis (that ‚Äúheterosexual women show stronger preferences for uncommitted sexual relationships [with more masculine men] during the high-fertility ovulatory phase of the menstrual cycle, while preferring long-term relationships at other points‚Äù). Studies are usually tiny (median n=34, mostly over one cycle). Funnel plot looks ok though.

    Statistics

    • Original paper: ‘Menstrual cycle variation in women’s preferences for the scent of symmetrical men’, Gangestad and Thornhill (1998). (602 citations).
    • Critiques: Jones et al (2018) (total citations: 32)
    • Original effect size: g = 0.15, SE = 0.04, n=5471 in the meta-analysis. Massive battery of preferences included (…)
    • Replication effect size: Not a meta-analysis, just a list of recent well-conducted “null” studies and a plausible alternative explanation.
    • Note from a professor friend: the idea of a dual-mating hypothesis itself is not in trouble: the specific menstrual cycle research doesn’t seem to replicate well. However, to my knowledge the basic pattern of short vs long term relationship goals predicting [women’s] masculinity preferences is still robust.

  • Men‚Äôs strength in particular predicts opposition to egalitarianism.

    Statistics

    • Original paper: Petersen et al (194 citations).
    • Critiques: Measurement was of arm circumference in students, and effect disappeared when participant age is included. (total citations: 605)
    • Original effect size: N/A, battery of F-tests.
    • Replication effect size: Gelman: none as in zero. The same lab later returned with 12 conceptual replications on a couple of measures of (anti-)egalitarianism. They are very focussed on statistical significance instead of effect size. Overall male effect was b = 0.17 and female effect was b = 0.11, with a nonsignificant difference between the two (p = 0.09). (They prefer to emphasise the lab studies over the online studies, which showed a stronger difference.) Interesting that strength or “formidability” has an effect in both genders, whether or not their main claim about gender difference holds up.


Psychophysiology

  • Sympathetic nervous system activity predicts political ideology. In particular, subjects‚Äô skin conductance reaction to threatening or disgusting visual prompts.

    Statistics

    • Original paper: Oxley et al, n=46 ( citations). p=0.05 on a falsely binarised measure of ideology.
    • Critiques: Six replications so far (Knoll et al; 3 from Bakker et al), five negative as in nonsignificant, one forking (“holds in US but not Denmark”) (total citations: )
    • Original effect size:
    • Replication effect size:

Applied Linguistics

  • Critical period hypothesis. How grammar-learning ability changes with age, finding that it is intact to the crux of adulthood (17.4 years) and then declines steadily.

    Statistics

    • Status: not replicated
    • Original paper: A critical period for second language acquisition: Evidence from 2/3 million English speakers**, **Hartshorne et al., 2018; n = 669,498 [citations = 313 (GS Feb 2022)].
    • Critique: van der Silk et al., (2021)[n = 547,250, citations = 1 (GS, Feb 2022)].
    • Original effect size: N/A
    • Replication effect size: N/A

  • Motivational role of L2 vision. Mental imagery of oneself as a successful language user in the future can enhance one‚Äôs motivation and performance.

    Statistics

    • Status: not replicated
    • Original paper: Motivation, vision and gender, You et al. (2016); n=10,569 [citation=172(GS, Oct 2022)]‚Äã.
    • Critiques: Hiver and Al-Hoorie (2020) [n=1297, citations=36(GS, Oct 2022)].
    • Original effect size:Œ∑2 =0.08
    • Replication effect size: N/A


Educational Psychology

  • Flipped learning, students learn better if they do homework about a lesson before coming to class to study that lesson.

    Statistics

    • Status: replicated
    • Original paper: Flip Your Classroom: Reach Every Student in Every Class Every Day. [citation=6585(Google Scholar, Dec 2021)]‚Äã.
    • Critiques: Lo & Hew [citations=423(Google Scholar, Dec 2021)], Strelan et al. [n=33678, citation=107(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], Cheng et al. [n=7912, citation=195(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], L√•g & S√¶le [n=not reported, number of reports=272, citation=106(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], Lo & Hew [n=5329, citation=43(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], Shi et al. [n=6947, citation=60(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], van Altren et al. [n=24771, citation=239(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], Xu et al. [n=4295, citation=33(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)], Vitta & Al-Hoorie [n=4220, citation=17(Google Scholar, Jan 2022)].
    • Meta-analysis effect size: Strelan et al.: g = 0.50 (0.42-0.52) cross-disciplinary. Cheng et al.: g = 0.19 (0.11, 0.27)‚Äã cross-disciplinary. L√•g and S√¶le: g = 0.35 (0.31, 0.40) cross-disciplinary. Lo & Hew: g = .29 (0.17, 0.41) engineering education. Shi et al.: g = 0.53 (0.36, 0.70) cross-disciplinary. van Altren et al.: g = 0.36 (0.28, 0.44) cross-disciplinary. Xu et al.: d = 1.79 (1.32, 2.27) nursing education in China. Vitta & Al-Hoorie: g = 0.99 (0.81, 1.16) second language learning. In Vitta & Al-Hoorie‚Äôs study, Trim and Fill suggested possible publication bias inflating the results, but the adjusted effect size remained sizable: g = 0.58 (0.37, 0.78).

  • Mindsets, people‚Äôs beliefs about whether their talents and abilities are subject to growth and improvement. According to the meta-analysis by Sisk and colleagues ( 2018), the relationship between mindsets and academic achievement is weak: Of the 129 studies that they analyzed, only 37% found a positive relationship between mindset and academic outcomes. Furthermore, 58% of the studies found no relationship and 6% found a negative relationship between mindset and academic outcomes. Evidence on the efficacy of mindset interventions is not promising: of the 29 studies reviewed, only 12% had a positive effect, 86% of the studies found no effect of the intervention and 2% found a negative effect of the intervention. It should be noted that interventions seemed to work for low SES populations.

    Statistics

    • Status: mixed
    • Original paper: ‚ÄòImplicit Theories of Intelligence Predict Achievement Across an Adolescent Transition‚Äô, Blackwell, Trzesniewski,& Dweck, 2007. Study 1: longitudinal study, n=373; Study 2: n=99 [citation=4643(GS, Oct 2021)]‚Äã.
    • Critiques: Li and Bates 2017
    • Original effect size: (between growth mindset and achievement) Œ≤=.10-.70, r=.12-.20
    • Replication effect size:


Health Psychology

  • Stress as the main/sole cause of peptic ulcers. Until the 1980s, stress was believed to be the main cause of peptic ulcers (with secondary contributing factors thought to eb excess stomach acid, spicy food). There may be some effect/role of stress involved in development and/or healing of ulcers but the evidence shows it is not the primary cause as was previously believed.

    Statistics

    • Status:
    • Original paper:
    • Critiques: Marshall & Warren (1983).https://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(84)91816-6/fulltext The bacterium H. pylori identified as correlated with ulcers.
    • Subsequent researchers showed treating ulcers with antibiotics reduced recurrence by approx 90-95% (Gough et al. 1984 https://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(84)91224-8/fulltext ). Stress/distress is still thought to be a contributing factor to development of ulcers (Levenstein et al. 1997; Levenstein et al. 1999; Overmeier & Murison 1997), as well as poorer response to treatment(Levenstein et al 1996), but is now recognised as not being the main or only influence.

Political Psychology

  • Stereotype threat on gender differences in political knowledge, the idea that making gender stereotypes about political knowledge salient decrease womens‚Äô performance on political knowledge tests. The replication effort showed no significant effect of gender stereotype activation on womens‚Äô performance on a political knowledge test. ‚Äã

    Statistics

    • Status: Not replicated.
    • Original paper: Gender Differences in Political Knowledge: Bringing Situation Back In, Ihme & Tausendpfund (2018). Study 1: N= 603, shows that women are rated as less politically knowledgeable than men. Study 2: N=377; Female and male participants are randomly assigned to one of three conditions (stereotype not activated - control, stereotype activated by asking participants to report their gender, stereotype activated by a statement that there are gender differences in performance on the test participants are about to take) and answer a questionnaire assessing political knowledge. [citation=17 (GS, January 2022)]‚Äã.
    • Critiques: Azevedo, Micheli & Bolesta [Preprint] [n=1502, citations=NA]. Results showed a non-significant interaction between stereotype activation and gender on political knowledge scores.
    • Original effect size: partial Œ∑2 =0.33‚Äã.
    • Replication effect size: partial Œ∑2 =0.00‚Äã.

Evolutionary Linguistics

  • Typological Prevalence Hypothesis. Claims that cross-linguistically more prevalent distinctions are easier to learn, or the more common a certain distinction or way of categorizing across languages, the more cognitively natural (and easily learnable) for humans it should be. This effect is explored for the grammatical structure of evidentiality, or grammatical marking of information source in an utterance.

    Statistics

    • Status: mixed
    • Original paper: ‚ÄòAre cross-linguistically frequent semantic systems easier to learn? The case of evidentiality‚Äô, Saratsli et al. 2019; between-group study design, n1=34, n2=34, n3=33. [citation=1(GS, June 2022)]‚Äã.
    • Critiques: Saratsli et al. 2020 [n1=111, n2=101, n3=98; citations=13(GS, June 2022)]. Saratsli & Papafragou 2021 [n=64, citation=0(GS, June 2022)]. Ilchovska & Culbertson 2019 [n=36, citations=0(GS, June 2022)].
    • Original effect size: N/A
    • Replication effect size: Saratsli et al. (2020): NA. Saratsli & Papafragou: NA. Ilchovska & Culbertson: NA.

Speech Language Therapy

  • Bilingualism and stuttering, Bilingual children had an increased risk of stuttering and a lower chance of recovery from stuttering than language exclusive and monolingual speakers.

    Statistics

    • Status: NA
    • Original paper: ‚ÄòThe effects of bilingualism on stuttering during late childhood‚Äô. Howell et al. (2009). n= 317[citations = 122, (GS, February, 2022)].
    • Critiques: Packman et al. 2009 [citations =23, (GS, February 2022)].
    • Original effect size: Cramer‚Äôs V = 0.34.
    • Replication effect size: NA

  • Self esteem and stuttering. Children who stutter have higher self-esteem than children who do not stutter. However, the self-esteem of children who stutter declines once they reach adolescence.

    Statistics

    • Status: NA
    • Original paper: Selbstwert von stotternden Kindern und Jugendlichen (in German). Z√ºckner (2011). Case-control study - comparison against norm scores with n = 171[citations = 3, (GS, February 2022)].
    • Critiques: Cook and Howell 2014 [observational, n=59, citations=16, (GS, February 2022)]
    • Original effect size: M(SD)stuttering boys = 56.5 (25.9), M(SD)boys normgroup: 36.5(25.9); M(SD)stuttering girls = 43.1(35.8), M(SD)girls normgroup=27.7(25.7)
    • Replication effect size: Cook and Howell M(SD) = 2.9(0.49) (children: adolescent: r(bullying, self-esteem = .387)


Experimental Philosophy

  • Fake barn cases. ‚ÄúOlder participants are less likely than younger participants to attribute knowledge in fake-barn cases‚Äù

    Statistics

    • Status: not replicated
    • Original paper: Epistemic Intuitions in Fake-Barn Thought Experiments, Cola√ßo et al. (2014) between subjects n=234 (n=85 in the relevant analysis) [citation=181 (GS, October 2022)] Critiques: Bergenholtz et al. (2021) [n=348, citations=0 (GS, October 2022)]
    • Original effect size: r = ‚àí0.32
    • Replication effect size: No effect size given (because of non-significant effect)


Further literature

  • Multi-Analyst Projects collated by Balazs Aczel

  • A review of 2500 social science papers, showing the lack of correlation between citations and replicability, between journal status and replicability, and the apparent lack of improvement since 2009.

  • Diederick Stapel: 58 retractions here.

  • Discussion on Everything Hertz, Hacker News, Andrew Gelman, some star data thugs comment.

  • See also the popular literature with uncritical treatments of the original studies:

    • Outliers: The Story of Success by Malcolm Gladwell that is founded on the 10,000 hours for mastery claim.
    • Behave: The Biology of Humans at Our Best and Worst by Robert Sapolsky which focuses on Himmicanes, power pose, facial feedback, ego depletion, Implicit Association, stereotype threat, broken windows theory, Macbeth effect.
    • Thinking, Fast and Slow by Daniel Kahneman that has an Entire chapter on all kinds of priming. Facial-feedback, Effects of Head-Movements on Persuasion, Location as Prime, Money Priming, Death Priming, Lady Macbeth Effect. Cognitive disfluency. Ego depletion. Wansink. Hungry judges. Denies the “hot hand”.
    • Nudge: Improving Decisions about Health, Wealth, and Happiness by Thaler and Sunstein focusing on Wansink, Baumeister, Dweck.
    • Smarter: The New Science of Building Brain Power by Dan Hurley focusing on Dual n-Back
    • Peter Watts Is An Angry Sentient Tumor: Revenge Fantasies and Essays by Peter Watts
